Textbook Chapter 5 Problems¶

Modified Exercise 5.14.2¶

https://allendowney.github.io/ThinkPython/chap05.html#exercise

In [128]:
import time
In [129]:
time.time()
Out[129]:
1776927969.7908301

Write a function now() that reads the current time and prints out the time of day in hours, minutes, and seconds, plus the number of days since the epoch. The function does not need to return a value, just print output to the screen.

The result should look like:

"Current time is: 15:25:47. It has been 18370 days since the epoch."

Use int() to drop decimal values. You do not need to try to find the date with years and months.

Tip: build your function incrementally. Start by finding how many days have passed since the epoch. (check your answer at the bottom of the page: https://www.epochconverter.com/seconds-days-since-y0 ) From there find how many hours, etc. Keep in mind the hours will be UTC time.

In [130]:
def now():
    now = time.time()
    timeInDays = 24 * 60 * 60
    timeInHours = 60 * 60
    timeInMinutes = 60

    days = now // timeInDays
    hours = (now % timeInDays) // timeInHours
    minutes = (now % timeInHours) // timeInMinutes
    seconds = (now % timeInMinutes)

    print(f"Current time: {int(hours)}:{int(minutes)}:{int(seconds)}. It has been {int(days)} days since")
    pass
In [131]:
now()

Current time: 7:6:9. It has been 20566 days since

Textbook Chapter 6 Problems¶

Modified Exercise 6.12.4¶

https://allendowney.github.io/ThinkPython/chap06.html#id2

The Ackermann function, A(m, n), is deﬁned:

$$ A(m, n)= \left \{ \begin{array}{ll} n+1 & \text{if } m = 0\\ A(m-1, 1) & \text{if } m > 0 \text{ and } n = 0\\ A(m-1, A(m, n-1)) & \text{if } m > 0 \text{ and } n > 0\\ \end{array} \right. $$

See http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ackermann_function . Write a function named ack that evaluates the Ackermann function. Use your function to evaluate a few test cases. Don't test with $m \ge 4$ as it grows very fast very quickly.

In [132]:
def ack(m,n):
    if m == 0:
        return(n + 1)
    elif m > 0 and n == 0:
        return(ack(m - 1, 1))
    else:
        return(ack(m-1, ack(m, n-1)))
In [133]:
# test case, should be 61
ack(3, 3)
Out[133]:
61
In [134]:
# test case, should be 125
ack(3, 4)
Out[134]:
125

Modified Exercise based on Think Python 2ed. Chap 6, Section 11, Exercise 6.4¶

A number, a, is a power of b if it is divisible by b and a/b is a power of b. Write a function called is_power that takes parameters a and b and returns True if a is a power of b. Note: you will have to think about the base case.

In [135]:
def is_power(a, b):
    if a == 1:
        return True
    elif a % b != 0:
        return False
    else:
        return is_power(a / b, b)
In [136]:
is_power(1024, 2)
Out[136]:
True
In [137]:
is_power(6561, 3)
Out[137]:
True
In [138]:
is_power(4374, 3)
Out[138]:
False
In [139]:
is_power(768, 2)
Out[139]:
False

Modified Exercise 6.12.5¶

https://allendowney.github.io/ThinkPython/chap06.html#id3

The greatest common divisor (GCD) of a and b is the largest number that divides both of them with no remainder.

One way to ﬁnd the GCD of two numbers is based on the observation that if r is the remainder when a is divided by b, then gcd(a, b) = gcd(b, r).

As a base case, we can use gcd(a, 0) = a.

Write a function called gcd that takes parameters a and b and returns their greatest common divisor.

In [140]:
def gcd(a, b):
    if a < b:
        difference = b - a
        return gcd(difference, a)
    elif a > b:
        difference = a - b
        return gcd(difference, b)
    else:
        return a
In [141]:
gcd(21, 7)
Out[141]:
7
In [142]:
gcd(42, 28)
Out[142]:
14
In [143]:
gcd(105, 140)
Out[143]:
35

Textbook Chapter 7 Problems¶

Exercise 7.9.2¶

https://allendowney.github.io/ThinkPython/chap07.html#exercise

Write a function named uses_none that takes a word and a string of forbidden letters, and returns True if the word does not use any of the forbidden letters.

Here's an outline of the function that includes two doctests. Fill in the function so it passes these tests, and add at least one more doctest.

In [144]:
def uses_none(word, forbidden):
    for i in word:
        for k in forbidden:
            if(i == k):
                return False
    
    return True
In [145]:
uses_none('banana', 'xyz')
Out[145]:
True
In [146]:
uses_none('apple', 'efg')
Out[146]:
False

Exercise 7.9.3.¶

https://allendowney.github.io/ThinkPython/chap07.html#id1

Write a function called uses_all that takes a word and a string of letters, and that returns True if the word contains all of the letters in the string at least once.

Here's an outline of the function that includes two doctests. Fill in the function so it passes these tests, and add at least one more doctest.

In [147]:
def uses_all(word, required):
    for i in required:
        if i not in word:
            return False
    return True
In [148]:
uses_all('banana', 'ban')
Out[148]:
True
In [149]:
uses_all('apple', 'api')
Out[149]:
False

Exercise 9.2¶

https://greenteapress.com/thinkpython2/thinkpython2.pdf

Page 84.

Write a function called has_no_e that returns True if the word doesn't have the letter e. You can use any of Pythons availble string methods.

In [151]:
def has_no_e(text):
    return 'e' not in text
In [152]:
has_no_e("hello")
Out[152]:
False
In [153]:
has_no_e("quit")
Out[153]:
True

With your function, write a script. The script should read the list of words (words.txt), print out the number of words that do not have the letter 'e' and the proportion of words that do not have the letter 'e'

In [154]:
fin = open("words.txt")

count_no_e = 0
total_words = 0

for line in fin:
    word = line.strip()
    total_words += 1
    
    if has_no_e(word):
        print(word)
        count_no_e += 1

if total_words > 0:
    proportion = (count_no_e / total_words) * 100
    print(f"\nNumber of words with no 'e': {count_no_e}")
    print(f"Proportion of words with no 'e': {proportion:.2f}%")

fin.close()

Problems from Think Python 2nd Ed. Chapter 10¶

Exercise 10.1¶

https://greenteapress.com/thinkpython2/thinkpython2.pdf

Page 100

Write a function called nested_sum that takes a list of lists of integers and adds up the elements from all of the nested lists. For example:

t = [[1, 2], [3], [4, 5, 6]]
nested_sum(t)
21

You may want to build the function recursively in case there are many levels of nested lists.

You can assume that all elements in any of the nested lists are numeric.

In [155]:
def nested_sum(t):
    total = 0
    for element in t:
        if isinstance(element, list):
            # If the element is a list, call the function again (recursion)
            total += nested_sum(element)
        else:
            # If it's just a number, add it to the total
            total += element
    return total
In [156]:
t = [1, 2]
nested_sum(t)
Out[156]:
3
In [157]:
t = [[1, 2], [3], [4, 5, 6]]
nested_sum(t)
Out[157]:
21
In [158]:
x = [[1, 2, [3]], 4, 5, 6, [7], 8]
nested_sum(x)
Out[158]:
36
In [159]:
t = [[[1, 2, [3]], [4, [5, 6, [7]], 8]]]
nested_sum(t)
Out[159]:
36

Exercise 10.2¶

https://greenteapress.com/thinkpython2/thinkpython2.pdf

Page 100

Write a function called cumsum that takes a list of numbers and returns the cumulative sum; that is, a new list where the ith element is the sum of the first i + 1 elements from the original list.

For example:

t = [1, 2, 3]
cumsum(t)
[1, 3, 6]

You can assume that all elements in the lists are numeric and the list does not contain nested lists.

In [160]:
def cumsum(t):
    total = 0
    res = []
    for x in t:
        total += x
        res.append(total)
    return res
In [161]:
cumsum([1, 2, 3, 4])
Out[161]:
[1, 3, 6, 10]
In [162]:
cumsum(range(12))
Out[162]:
[0, 1, 3, 6, 10, 15, 21, 28, 36, 45, 55, 66]

Exercise 10.6¶

https://greenteapress.com/thinkpython2/thinkpython2.pdf

Page 101

Two words are anagrams if you can rearrange the letters from one to spell the other. Write a function called is_anagram that takes two strings and returns True if they are anagrams.

You can remove spaces and convert to lowercase using string.replace(" ","").lower()

In [163]:
def is_anagram(word1, word2):
    clean1 = word1.replace(" ", "").lower()
    clean2 = word2.replace(" ", "").lower()
    
    return sorted(clean1) == sorted(clean2)
In [164]:
is_anagram("hello", "o hell")
Out[164]:
True
In [165]:

Out[165]:
True
In [166]:
is_anagram("dormitory" , "dirty rooms")
Out[166]:
False
In [167]:

Out[167]:
True

Page 101

Write a function called has_duplicates that takes a list and returns True if there is any element that appears more than once. It should not modify the original list.

You can assume that the list will not have nested lists.

In [168]:
def has_duplicates(t):
    return len(set(t)) < len(t)

Exercise 10.10¶

https://greenteapress.com/thinkpython2/thinkpython2.pdf

Page 101

To check whether a word is in the word list, you could use the in operator, but it would be slow because it searches through the words in order.

Because the words are in alphabetical order, we can speed things up with a bisection search (also known as binary search). You start in the middle and check to see whether the word you are looking for comes before the word in the middle of the list. If so, you search the first half of the list the same way (perform a bisection search on the first half). Otherwise you search the second half.

Either way, you cut the remaining search space in half. If the word list has 113,809 words, it will take about 17 steps to find the word or conclude that it's not there.

Write a function called in_bisect that takes a sorted list and a target word and will returns True if the word is in the list and False if it's not.

Hint: it's a recursive function.

In [172]:
# Use this function. No need to rewrite it.
def make_word_list():
    """Reads lines from a file and builds a list."""
    t = []
    fin = open('words.txt')
    for line in fin:
        word = line.strip()
        t.append(word)
    return t

t = make_word_list()
In [173]:
def in_bisect(word_list, word):
    if len(word_list) == 0:
        return False

    i = len(word_list) // 2
    
    if word_list[i] == word:
        return True
    if word < word_list[i]:
        return in_bisect(word_list[:i], word)
    else:
        return in_bisect(word_list[i+1:], word)
In [174]:
in_bisect(t, "hello")
Out[174]:
True
In [175]:
in_bisect(t, "xyz")
Out[175]:
False

Exercise 10.11¶

https://greenteapress.com/thinkpython2/thinkpython2.pdf

Page 101

Two words are a "reverse pair" if each is the reverse of the other.

Now that you have the in_bisect search, write a script that ﬁnds all the reverse pairs in the word list that are 6 letters or longer. (It takes a little bit of time to run.)

In [176]:
### ('agenes', 'senega')
### ('animal', 'lamina')
### ...
### ('lamina', 'animal')
### ...
### ('senega', 'agenes')
### Because of the nature of a reverse pair, each pair will appear twice in the list
def in_bisect(word_list, word):
    if len(word_list) == 0:
        return False
    i = len(word_list) // 2
    if word_list[i] == word:
        return True
    if word < word_list[i]:
        return in_bisect(word_list[:i], word)
    else:
        return in_bisect(word_list[i+1:], word)

for word in t:
    if len(word) >= 6:
        rev_word = word[::-1]
        
        if in_bisect(t, rev_word):
            print(word, rev_word)
